<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_243848184">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/243848184" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows" class="left imgcol" title="Jess ?Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows?"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1440512431p2/7125124.jpg" alt="Jess ?Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows?" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/243848184?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows" class="user" title="Jess ?Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows?">Jess ?Harbinger of Blood-Soaked Rainbows?</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/14786924-wicked" title="Paperback " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=book-club-picks" class="actionLinkLite">book-club-picks</a>,

              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=own-it" class="actionLinkLite">own-it</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=here-be-witches" class="actionLinkLite">here-be-witches</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=2-and-a-half-stars" class="actionLinkLite">2-and-a-half-stars</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=retellings-and-such" class="actionLinkLite">retellings-and-such</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=the-rory-list" class="actionLinkLite">the-rory-list</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7125124-jess-harbinger-of-blood-soaked-rainbows?shelf=popsugar-2015" class="actionLinkLite">popsugar-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer243848184">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16890222130335673693"><img width="375" height="250" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1430800232i/14764230._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><strong>Read a book with nonhuman characters.<br /><br />2.5 stars</strong><br /><br /><em> <blockquote>"'Listen to me, sister,' she said. 'Remember this: Nothing is written in the stars. Not these stars, nor any others. No one controls your destiny.'"</blockquote> </em><br /><br />I have never ever been more frustrated while reading a book. As most of you know from my continuous status updates describing my turmoil at my insistence that I NEVER EVER DNF anything, I had a really hard time getting into this one. It was boring and academic, and I would constantly find myself falling</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16890222130335673693"><img width="375" height="250" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1430800232i/14764230._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><strong>Read a book with nonhuman characters.<br /><br />2.5 stars</strong><br /><br /><em>
  <blockquote>"'Listen to me, sister,' she said. 'Remember this: Nothing is written in the stars. Not these stars, nor any others. No one controls your destiny.'"</blockquote>
</em><br /><br />I have never ever been more frustrated while reading a book. As most of you know from my continuous status updates describing my turmoil at my insistence that I NEVER EVER DNF anything, I had a really hard time getting into this one. It was boring and academic, and I would constantly find myself falling asleep while reading. Excuse me, Mr. Maguire, but when you want to market your book as fantasy, please give your book a plot. A real plot involving action and intrigue and drama. Not pages and pages and pages of description of political plots that make absolutely no sense. I wanted to like this book so much. SO MUCH. But it's such a case of an amazing premise being swallowed whole by an author's ambition and preoccupation with style.<br /><img width="250" height="190" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380413982ra/815775.gif" /><br /><br />On the plus side, I really loved the character of Elphaba. She was strong and intelligent and never strayed from her values and beliefs. Her romance with Fiyero was very deep and realistically portrayed, and the emotions and feelings that tied into that relationship were my favorite part of the novel. And that's saying something because of me and my you know, cold black cynical heart. And the romantic subplot only lasted for maybe 40-50 pages of the entire novel but made the most lasting impression on me. Some of the supporting characters, such as Fiyero and Boq were interesting and dynamic enough also, but for the most part, the other characters were pretty flat.<br /><br />God, did I say how disappointing this novel was? Because there were moments of sheer brilliance within these pages, but most of the time I was just so effing bored. When Maguire was on, he was very on. And the satire and political and social commentary was so good and interesting and thought-provoking. Things like this:<br /><em>
  <blockquote>"The more civilized we become, the more horrendous our entertainments."</blockquote>
</em><br />And<br /><em>
  <blockquote>"'The real thing about evil,' said the Witch at the doorway, 'isn't any of what you said. You figure out one side of it--the human side, say--and the eternal side goes into shadow. Or, vice-versa. It's like the old saw: What does a dragon in its shell look like? Well no one can ever tell, for as soon as you break the shell to see, the dragon is no longer in its shell. The real disaster of this inquiry is that it is the nature of evil to be secret.'"</blockquote>
</em><br />But every single time I caught some brilliance here, it was countered by boring writing, lack of imagination, and bizarre world-building. I even read <a title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz (Oz, #1) by L. Frank Baum" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/236093.The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz__Oz___1_">the original tale</a> in order to get an idea of the original world this novel was built on, and I became even more confused by it. I am hoping that a read of Baum's other Oz tales may shed some more light on his rendering of Oz. I certainly hope so. There were people and events mentioned in this book that were never explained and that frustrated the hell out of me. If they aren't important to the story, WHY HAVE THEM TAKE UP SPACE? UGH!<br /><br />And seriously? The conflict between the witch and Dorothy was only because Glinda gave Dorothy the shoes that Elphaba wanted? And Elphaba only wanted them because her father made them and they were promised to her. Seriously??? Well, I have read this one and the original, and I loved Elphaba more than I ever loved Dorothy so, I kinda think that Dorothy's a bitch now. Take that, Judy Garland! You shoulda just given her the damn shoes.<br /><img width="398" height="241" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380404838r/788149._SX540_SY540_.gif" /><br /><br />All in all, this book frustrated me and confused me and gave me a headache, but it wasn't all bad. There was some juicy meat, but Maguire's dry style of writing made it hard to get to. I have heard from several friends both on here and in real life that <a title="Son of a Witch (The Wicked Years #2) by Gregory Maguire" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13521.Son_of_a_Witch__The_Wicked_Years__2_">Son of a Witch</a> is much much better than this one, so I think I will give it a shot at some point.<br /><br />And we all know what happens to the Wicked Witch, so the ending is not a spoiler, but I was upset nonetheless. Why do we have to hate on people who are different? Who stand up for themselves and for those who can't fight? Why do we love the beautiful and hate the ugly? Elphaba, you get on with your bad green self. You made this girl love you.<br /><img width="245" height="184" class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1392745133ra/8604395.gif" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16890222130335673693">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16277674" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/934928-danny" class="left imgcol" title="Danny"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1203881827p2/934928.jpg" alt="Danny" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16277674?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 04, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/934928-danny" class="user" title="Danny">Danny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/37442.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060987107" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16277674">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3079762734802962947">This is one of my favorites. I generally like McGuire's books, but in my opinion this is by far the best of them. <br /><br />After reading my teenaged years away through endless sci-fi/fantasy books where the characters are often painfully stereotypical "golden boys" (and they are almost always boys), it is refreshing to encounter a book where the protagonist is deeply flawed, but yet we respect her. The characters and interpersonal relationships in this book are complicated but leave you guessing about de</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3079762734802962947">This is one of my favorites. I generally like McGuire's books, but in my opinion this is by far the best of them. <br /><br />After reading my teenaged years away through endless sci-fi/fantasy books where the characters are often painfully stereotypical "golden boys" (and they are almost always boys), it is refreshing to encounter a book where the protagonist is deeply flawed, but yet we respect her. The characters and interpersonal relationships in this book are complicated but leave you guessing about details that occur behind the scenes and curious about the tales that are left untold. <br /><br />This is not to say that the complicated style never has its frustrations. There are occasional scenes which I still do not really understand, but for the most part these have merely increased the "re-readability" of this novel and kept me on my toes while reading. The story also does not make for easy reading due to some of these complications, and I could see the story turning off readers who might have been misled by the apparently fairy-tale-esq premise of the book into expecting something else.<br /><br />McGuire slips in some of his philosophy seamlessly into the dialog and actions of the characters. There were moments when I would read through a sentence of apparently innocuous text, only to do a mental double-take seconds later, struck by how easily a profound statement was weaved into the story without pomp or preamble.<br /><br />In my opinion, at the very least this is an entertaining book if you have ever watched The Wizard of Oz. But I also find it to be much more than just a good yarn.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3079762734802962947">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_44606713">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44606713" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/160319-seth-t" class="left imgcol" title="Seth T."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1251915636p2/160319.jpg" alt="Seth T." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/44606713?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 07, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/160319-seth-t" class="user" title="Seth T.">Seth T.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/37442.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060987107" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/160319-seth-t?shelf=bookclub" class="actionLinkLite">bookclub</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer44606713">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17445142989663141025">I had the vaguely unique opportunity to approach MacGuire's most famous work without any immediate familiarity with his source material. I had neither read L. Baum's original work nor seen the Judy Garland vehicle. Certainly, some of the Oz mythos has filtered down into society at large over the years and I am broadly aware of some of the stories more famous bits.<br /><br />I knew of the yellow brick road—upon which I presumed the entire tale took place. I knew of the ruby slippers, though little more than</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17445142989663141025">I had the vaguely unique opportunity to approach MacGuire's most famous work without any immediate familiarity with his source material. I had neither read L. Baum's original work nor seen the Judy Garland vehicle. Certainly, some of the Oz mythos has filtered down into society at large over the years and I am broadly aware of some of the stories more famous bits.<br /><br />I knew of the yellow brick road—upon which I presumed the entire tale took place. I knew of the ruby slippers, though little more than that such shoes were extant. I knew that Dorothy arrived in a house that crushed a witch and have in mind the image of two spindly legs clothed in candy-cane-striped socks emerged from beneath its deadly weight. I knew of a quest for a heart, a brain, and some other thing. Perhaps a spleen. I knew that the villain melted and suspected it was due to some sort of molecular aquaphobia. And I knew that one ought pay no attention to the man behind the curtain.<br /><br />Oh. And that flying monkeys apparently played some role.<br /><br />With that in mind, I can say that MacGuire crafts a tale that takes a path down a road that with few exceptions is easy enough for even an Oz Neophyte to follow without getting lost. I did occasionally wonder what happened to seemingly significant characters with whom the narrative seemed to grow weary and decided to abandon outright, never to be seen again. Case file: the Scarecrow and the Woodsman. Both seem to carry some narrative force and even feature prominently in the prologue. But Macguire leaves them apart and away from Dorothy and Elphaba near the climax and never returns to them or their presumed plight. I was not quite sure of their function in the story then save perhaps because lore required their presence.<br /><br />But other wise, yes. <em>Wicked</em> carves a narrative path that is simple enough for even a newcomer to follow. Unfortunately, the path—which began in earnest and promised great sights and breathtaking vistas—becomes dull and plodding and any number of things that a desirable trek would hope to avoid. <br /><br />Plainly then? <em>Wicked</em> loses steam in its first third and never regains its spirit. It becomes, in a word, boring.<br /><br />I was engaged-if-perplexed in the first section, in which we are introduced to the infant Elphaba and her deleteriously bankrupt family. I was excited throughout her college years as she first learns to relate (or attempts to do so) with classmates and experiences tastes of the wider world of ideas and ideals. This second section of MacGuire's journey is easily his strongest work here and he creates a likable, mischievous cast with which Elphaba might parry and riposte. He hints at great things to come and struggles for which Elphaba and her friends have only just begun to take up arms. <br /><br />Unfortunately, it all falls apart immediately upon Elphaba's abandonment of her education.<br /><br />Not only does Elphie cease to develop in constructive ways, but her story becomes a shambles, never gaining the traction required to make the reader care. Oh, there are moments that hint of the things that could have been—the elephant queen, Liir's involvement with the great fish, Nor's experience of flight. Alas, these all remain as undeveloped and unevolved as the philosophical content at which the novel toys (the level of thoughtfulness is comparable to what one might find in a dormroom in which five college sophomores launch into outspoken dissertations on the nature of our reality fueled only by second-rate marijuana and a subconscious desire to feel important).<br /><br />MacGuire's <em>Wicked</em> is, in the final reckoning, deeply unsatisfying. Which is really too bad as the book is as long as a <em>Harry Potter</em> tome—only minus the fun.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17445142989663141025">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer1722368880914266908">Dostoyevsky meets Frank Baum. <br /><br />This was not as much fun as <a title="Pride and Prejudice and Zombies (Pride and Prejudice and Zombies, #1) by Seth Grahame-Smith" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5899779.Pride_and_Prejudice_and_Zombies__Pride_and_Prejudice_and_Zombies___1_">Pride and Prejudice and Zombies</a> maybe because it is more revisionist than mash-up, parody or spoof. <br /><br />This is actually, strangely a very mature work but set in Oz and from the perspective of Elpheba the wicked witch. But it was amusing, entertaining, thought provoking, head scratching, etc etc.<br /><br />Published 2 years before Harry Potter and 4 years before Phantom Menace, the influence on both series is apparent. <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_6368023">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6368023" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/66498-marigold" class="left imgcol" title="Marigold"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1187330130p2/66498.jpg" alt="Marigold" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/6368023?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 17, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/66498-marigold" class="user" title="Marigold">Marigold</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/37442.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060987107" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer6368023">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3637010870794766261">Very interesting book! Lots to like, and lots to dislike, but overall I enjoyed it, definitely wanted to keep reading, and found a lot to think about. What struck me most is the story of Elphaba alone. She starts life as an outcast for various reasons, &amp; really never recovers, though she's bright, determined, brave, a good friend and caring family member, and in some ways sensitive, though self-involved. The book seems to cast her as someone of great promise despite her rough start in life.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3637010870794766261">Very interesting book! Lots to like, and lots to dislike, but overall I enjoyed it, definitely wanted to keep reading, and found a lot to think about. What struck me most is the story of Elphaba alone. She starts life as an outcast for various reasons, &amp; really never recovers, though she's bright, determined, brave, a good friend and caring family member, and in some ways sensitive, though self-involved. The book seems to cast her as someone of great promise despite her rough start in life. She's a star pupil and an activist, and seemingly a woman who can attract intelligent and powerful men. Then her lover dies &amp; she really never gets over it. She spends years retired from the world, then searches out her lover's widow seeking forgiveness, which she never receives in a way that she can understand. She is followed by a little boy, who it seems is her son - and her dead lover the father - tho I'm not sure what the author is trying to say by making the son so much less than the sum of his parents! From this point Elphaba seems to deteriorate mentally. She becomes obsessed with recovering the ruby slippers and she wishes only to retire from life after getting them. She really doesn't accomplish anything with her life, which I found very sad. She pushes all her friends away, &amp; her family members mostly die before she does. So why is she wicked? Because she doesn't follow the conventional path that Glinda does? I have an odd feeling that Maguire sort of punished Elphaba for having a happy sexual life with her lover. Elphaba's mother is portrayed as a woman with loose sexual morals, &amp; look what happens to her - her children are born deformed in various ways, her husband mostly ignores her, her lover dies, she lives in squalor, &amp; dies in childbirth! Ouch! <br />There's a lot of interesting background about Oz, its politics, sociology, &amp; religions. I would have liked some of this more clearly laid out in the book. A lot of it felt pencilled in, &amp; this makes it appear less interesting. I think Maguire could have made a more fully realized fantasy world; it feels like he backs away from it by only giving bits &amp; pieces. There's discussion of the nature of good &amp; evil that is a bit pedantic - but where he shows "good" and "evil" through Glinda &amp; Elphaba, that's much more interesting because their portrayals mirror the relative nature of these concepts. Is Glinda good because she makes something of herself in the sense that she marries well, has money &amp; social position, gives money to good causes, etc? Is Elphaba wicked because she's not interested in those things? Or is she wicked because she doesn't manage to accomplish anything "good"? Are the fates of these women pre-determined in classic fairy tale style, because one is ugly and the other beautiful? What about Elphaba's sister, the other "wicked witch"? Is she wicked because she is self righteous &amp; wants to push her religious agenda? What does it mean that she's beautiful but literally armless? It's all very interesting. <br />On the other hand, the book is quite uneven - really well written in parts, then poorly written in others; unevenly paced so that parts feel very slow while other parts seem rushed; some details are very clear &amp; sometimes overly belabored, other details are entirely left out, leaving you wondering what happened! Characters who are very important in one part of the book, end up becoming minor players, and the other way around. While I realize that this may be art reflecting life, I have to admit that I usually enjoy some "wrapping up of loose ends" when I'm reading! There's an implication that Elphaba's father may have been The Wizard, but that's all we find out about that - this is just one example of a loose end, there are many others throughout the book. <br />Like Elphaba, this book is beautiful in its way, &amp; oddly fascinating - but somehow flawed and unfinished. But I'm very glad I read it &amp; I'll probably read it again to pick up things I missed. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3637010870794766261">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_567709660">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/567709660" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450116936p2/6313408.jpg" alt="Jonathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/567709660?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 21, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/37442.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060987107" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=unfinished" class="actionLinkLite">unfinished</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer567709660">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2364603158672026215"><br />I must state that there appears to be a particular type of novel which does not draw my fancy. <em>Wicked</em> happens to be that type of novel. That said I was expecting more from what I did read.<br /><br />With such a novel proclaimed as an imaginative retelling of <a title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz by L. Frank Baum" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/236093.The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz">The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</a> and a popular bestseller I felt I owed it to myself to give this a try. However I find similar issues with this novel as with the second of G.R.R Martin's <em>A Song of Fire and Ice</em> novels. In other words there feels a deliberate</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2364603158672026215"><br />I must state that there appears to be a particular type of novel which does not draw my fancy. <em>Wicked</em> happens to be that type of novel. That said I was expecting more from what I did read.<br /><br />With such a novel proclaimed as an imaginative retelling of <a title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz by L. Frank Baum" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/236093.The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz">The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</a> and a popular bestseller I felt I owed it to myself to give this a try. However I find similar issues with this novel as with the second of G.R.R Martin's <em>A Song of Fire and Ice</em> novels. In other words there feels a deliberate attempt in this novel to aim to write a gritty narrative for the purpose of selling the novel, rather than merely writing a strong story. The story attempts to create a modernist version of Oz but ignores what its origins set out to achieve. Further, it sexualises the world of Oz and adds in elements which frankly make little sense in the storyline or in the context of what it states that it is doing.<br /><br />In general, my conclusion from what I read of this book in skimming through is that it does not suit my taste. If one appreciates this novel then, well, that is certainly an individual choice. However I for one find it to be a little bit of a commercial gimmick, an aim to rewrite a classic story for the sake of selling novels and spreading an ideology (though I did not read enough to observe that ideology). As such I'd rather read the original tried and true classics rather than a work such as this.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2364603158672026215">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1137847">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1137847" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/81710-amy" class="left imgcol" title="Amy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1178783126p2/81710.jpg" alt="Amy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1137847?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 16, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/81710-amy" class="user" title="Amy">Amy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/37442.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060987107" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/81710-amy?shelf=favorite-author" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-author</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/81710-amy?shelf=read-multiple-times" class="actionLinkLite">read-multiple-times</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/81710-amy?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/81710-amy?shelf=broadway-awesomeness" class="actionLinkLite">broadway-awesomeness</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1137847">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18079623108109105667">I received my copy of this book as a Christmas present from a teacher and friend, and I had no idea that it would turn out to be one of my favorite books and favorite authors. My mother had brought me up loving the stories where the told the other side of the tale, that tried to explain that things aren't so black and white, and maybe there's a good reason for the "villain" to be doing what she's doing. Things like Fractured Fairy Tales from Rocky and Bullwinkle, and <a title=" The True Story of the Three Little Pigs" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20True%20Story%20of%20the%20Three%20Little%20Pigs"> The True Story of the Three</a></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18079623108109105667">I received my copy of this book as a Christmas present from a teacher and friend, and I had no idea that it would turn out to be one of my favorite books and favorite authors. My mother had brought me up loving the stories where the told the other side of the tale, that tried to explain that things aren't so black and white, and maybe there's a good reason for the "villain" to be doing what she's doing. Things like Fractured Fairy Tales from Rocky and Bullwinkle, and <a title=" The True Story of the Three Little Pigs" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20True%20Story%20of%20the%20Three%20Little%20Pigs"> The True Story of the Three Little Pigs</a> were what I'd grown up with.<br /><br />Wicked does an awesome job of painting a back story, motive and reason for misunderstanding and mistrust that takes the Wicked Witch of the West from being a two dimensional villain in a children's book, and turns her into a person you can understand and maybe even sympathize with. It adds layers of politics to this magical land of Oz, it transforms Glinda from being a fluffy happy ball of pink cotton candy with magical powers into someone who stepped into a role within the political game in the name of trying to balance things out. It remembers parts of the original book that the movie left out, it introduces us to characters we would have missed if we'd just seen the movie, it teases the parts of the movie we winced at (Glinda's pink dress, anyone?) and reconciles differences Hollywood saw fit to just throw out the window.<br /><br />In addition to being a book I so love, it also spawned a Broadway show with an awesome soundtrack that gets regular rotation in my music play list. ^_^</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18079623108109105667">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_11106176">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11106176" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/705590-francine" class="left imgcol" title="Francine"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1296600423p2/705590.jpg" alt="Francine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/11106176?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 24, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/705590-francine" class="user" title="Francine">Francine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who like driving all over the place, getting lost, and ending up in the middle of nowhere</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=books-i-don-t-care-for" class="actionLinkLite">books-i-don-t-care-for</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=modern-lit" class="actionLinkLite">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=american-lit" class="actionLinkLite">american-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=grown-up-fairy-tales" class="actionLinkLite">grown-up-fairy-tales</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=kindle" class="actionLinkLite">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer11106176">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10272524491935186122">I don't even know where to start. This book -- actually, the <em>concept</em> of this book -- had so much promise. This could've been a great revisionist retelling of the Wizard of Oz. Yes, it fleshed out Oz itself - what a rich land, people by various creatures: humans, animals and everything in between. The political and religious strata of Oz was well-thought out.<br /><br />But I couldn't connect to any of the characters, especially the main character of Elphaba. I felt that none of them were fleshed out. None o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10272524491935186122">I don't even know where to start. This book -- actually, the <em>concept</em> of this book -- had so much promise. This could've been a great revisionist retelling of the Wizard of Oz. Yes, it fleshed out Oz itself - what a rich land, people by various creatures: humans, animals and everything in between. The political and religious strata of Oz was well-thought out.<br /><br />But I couldn't connect to any of the characters, especially the main character of Elphaba. I felt that none of them were fleshed out. None of them were <em>likable</em>, either in a positive or negative way. Sometimes, you're drawn to characters who are so evil because they're interesting. You become interested in their back story (and there is always one) and what makes them tick. Of course, almost everyone is drawn to the hero of the story, be they good or flawed, regardless of whether they're the hero-type or the underdog. But in my mind, while Elphaba was the center of the story, there was just nothing about her that drew me in, nothing that made me want to know her better. Nothing tugged at my heart strings or made me go "Grrr...I can't stand her!"<br /><br />Maguire proselytized. Ad nauseum. To the point where I saw no point in going further with the book. While I can see that this work would appeal to some people who revel in exposition and live and breathe religious and political polemic (there are die-hard Wicked fans out there -- of the book, I mean, which is significantly different from the musical, and that has a large fan base as well) but sad to say, I am not one of them (a fan of the book). I think there's a place for everything, and when I pick up a book that purports to be about the life and times of the Wicked Witch of the West, I expect a fantastical backstory about <em>her</em>.<br /><br />And that's the thing. I know very little about her. She's green, but why is she green? She's got normal parents, but how did she become this freakish green baby with shark teeth? You know about her parents' dalliances, their religious fervor, their sexual proclivities, their societal vagaries. But you don't see how this shaped Elphaba. There are so many holes in the story, so many unanswered questions, and whenever I expected to find an answer, there were one or two vague, often nebulous, non-answers.<br /><br />The story meandered all over the place, dropping characters here and there into the narrative -- characters that (one hopes) will enrich the characterization of Elphaba's life. Sometimes they did; most times, they didn't. <br /><br />About a quarter of the way through, I wondered where this was all heading. About halfway through, I wanted to rip my hair out and beg for something -- anything -- to happen that would make me feel connected to our heroine or the story. But there was nothing. Not even a clock dragon to crawl into.<br /><br />This novel could've done so much. It held so much promise. So much. But it failed to deliver, and at least for me, it was a supreme letdown.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10272524491935186122">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_21164059">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21164059" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1118970-jamie" class="left imgcol" title="Jamie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1384277582p2/1118970.jpg" alt="Jamie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/21164059?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1118970-jamie" class="user" title="Jamie">Jamie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/10750.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060745905" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer21164059'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer21164059">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8714909527683476819">Just finished re-reading this as a sort of guilty-pleasure getaway between all my thesis readings. I guess it's been a couple of years since the last time I read it, because there was quite a bit I'd forgotten, and quite a bit that seems less dazzling to me than when I read it for the first, second, and even third time over the years. <br /><br />I'm not going to agree with a lot of reviewers here that the book is a piece of shit, that Maguire is a hack, that the novel should be on the Worst Literature of A</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8714909527683476819">Just finished re-reading this as a sort of guilty-pleasure getaway between all my thesis readings. I guess it's been a couple of years since the last time I read it, because there was quite a bit I'd forgotten, and quite a bit that seems less dazzling to me than when I read it for the first, second, and even third time over the years. <br /><br />I'm not going to agree with a lot of reviewers here that the book is a piece of shit, that Maguire is a hack, that the novel should be on the Worst Literature of All Time lists that have groups on goodreads. I love the novel in the way you can only love best-sellers and beach reads; if you go in expecting high literature, then you're pretty darned pretentious, if you ask me. Maguire may have a few lofty ambitions in the book, but the fact remains that he rewrote The Wizard of Oz from the witch's perspective. Both this novel and the original Oz series have some political, moral, and satirical undertones--but not with either of them did I go in expecting to feel like I was sifting my way through Faulkner or Joyce or Woolf. I think that's why it's frustrating to read the reviews on here, which seem to place labels and expectations on the book that the book never set out to fill. <br /><br />Sure, I definitely felt at some points that I could have written some of the prose (and done it better). Yes, a lot of the characters are a bit one-dimensional. And yes, there are some frustrating plot gaps. But it's still a thrilling read, and I love the premise behind the book so much that I feel a bit more lenient on other problems I might have with it. And some things are so innovative that Maguire actually deserves *some* credit--for example, I think it's fabulous that he takes something as minor as the odd markings on the soldiers at the Witch's palace (I know that was in the film, can't remember if Baum's version had the same soldiers) and creates a backstory (tribal markings) with a love interest (Fiyero), among other things. And then the whole animal/Animal distinction, and how that's perhaps an explanation for the Cowardly Lion. Nessarose's armlessness (almost funny in the context of seeing only shriveled legs in the original film), and the power of the ruby slippers to help her stand on her own two feet. There are problems with the novel, but I think the wonder and magic of it all outweigh the downfalls. Definitely worth it if you still have a glimmer of that child that watched The Wizard of Oz hundreds of times in youth (like I did).</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8714909527683476819">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_25351096">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25351096" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/84621-rachel" class="left imgcol" title="Rachel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1182846542p2/84621.jpg" alt="Rachel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/25351096?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 05, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84621-rachel" class="user" title="Rachel">Rachel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/37442.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060987107" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Adult readers who enjoyed reading L. Frank Baum's Oz books and are not offended by freaky sex scenes</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer25351096">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8087696713091702268">Reading this book was like returning to an old friend ... it was written in the style of the Oz books I had enjoyed as a kid, except now ... with X-RATED COMICAL PASSAGES! Woo-hoo! <br /><br />I bring this up because I see a lot of people posting that they take offense at the sexual nature of some of the passages. I think the author was trying to portray Oz as an "other world" loosely patterned after the European Renaissance (not entirely, of course: details such as train travel came later). From my underst</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8087696713091702268">Reading this book was like returning to an old friend ... it was written in the style of the Oz books I had enjoyed as a kid, except now ... with X-RATED COMICAL PASSAGES! Woo-hoo! <br /><br />I bring this up because I see a lot of people posting that they take offense at the sexual nature of some of the passages. I think the author was trying to portray Oz as an "other world" loosely patterned after the European Renaissance (not entirely, of course: details such as train travel came later). From my understanding, Shakespearean plays and other forms of entertainment at the time were quite bawdy. So I thought the sexual scenes (e.g., in that strange traveling peep show) fit in just fine with the tone of the book, providing an ominous backdrop for parts of the plot. L. Frank Baum's books didn't include any sexually expicit passages, but he was clearly writing more for general audiences (to include children). This book is written for ADULTS, and I think the sexual scenes definitely enhanced the chilling nature of the story. <br /><br />Lots of people have commented that the book was boring, but my experience was the OPPOSITE ... I couldn't put the darn thing down! I was intrigued by the character of Elphaba and everything going on in Oz around her. <br /><br />One thing I don't understand is how the author is promoting this book "Son of a Witch," (ahem: cute, cute) which is apparently based on the character of Liir. The author portrays Liir as a big bland boring doughboy. This makes NO SENSE given that the author is simultaenously trying to convince us to go out and buy his next book, which is all about said doughboy. Very strange. But whatever. <br /><br />One unexpected part of this book was Elphaba's allegiance toward animals and animal rights. At school, she speaks out against a professor who has taken a lion cub away from its mother for the purposes of biological experiments. (In the book, they have animals which are regular animals and Animals, capitalized, which are able to speak and think like humans.) Elphaba thinks its cruel to eat Animals, as she chides both her lover and her sister for eating meat that could possibly have been from an Animal rather than an animal. From what the book mentions of what Elphaba eats, she appears to be vegetarian. Elphaba does eat cheese, which, perhaps she is not aware, is just as harmful to Cows as it is to cows. Interestingly enough, the cruelty of milk and cheese production IS pointed out in the book, in a scene in which a Cow tells Elphaba about the pain of her life in the dairy business. Kudos to the author for that!!! <br /><br />ALL IN ALL, WICKED was a very enjoyable and captivating read. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8087696713091702268">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_6993856">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6993856" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/404424-helen" class="left imgcol" title="Helen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1191478671p2/404424.jpg" alt="Helen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/6993856?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 02, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/404424-helen" class="user" title="Helen">Helen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/37442.Wicked" title="Paperback 9780060987107" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who find the below description appealing</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Helen by: </span>
      The Witch of Fluff
  </div>



    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer6993856'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer6993856">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14142691447256097039">Woah. I'm still digesting this one. It was so fascinating, haunting, disgusting, inspiring, tragic, strange, funny, and danged confusing. And beautiful. This book is kind of in a genre of its own, a combination of psychological analysis, romance, religious theory, and mystery. And no, all those slick, poorly edited psychothriller-whodunit-we've-got-to-save-the-Pentagon-from-the-ticking-bomb-planted-by-the-maniac-you-met-in-the-first-chapter-because-he-thought-he-heard-God sort of things are <em>not</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14142691447256097039">Woah. I'm still digesting this one. It was so fascinating, haunting, disgusting, inspiring, tragic, strange, funny, and danged confusing. And beautiful. This book is kind of in a genre of its own, a combination of psychological analysis, romance, religious theory, and mystery. And no, all those slick, poorly edited psychothriller-whodunit-we've-got-to-save-the-Pentagon-from-the-ticking-bomb-planted-by-the-maniac-you-met-in-the-first-chapter-because-he-thought-he-heard-God sort of things are <em>not</em> in the same genre. (Ahem, Dan Brown, are you reading this?)<br /><br />[Spoiler Warning!]<br /><br />This book is really an in-depth psychological biography of the Wicked Witch. Turns out that the green one spent her entire life trying to figure out what life, death, good, bad, and God meant. It drove her crazy, especially when, towards the end, a mysterious little dwarf popped up (for the eighth random time) to tell her that she was essentially the product of the marriage between GOOD and EVIL themselves. Dang. So it was up to her to decide whether she had a soul or not, whether she was evil or not, whether whe was being manipulated by fate or not, etc. etc. She slowly descended into paranoia and that was when she met Dorothy, her death, and perhaps... her redemption with the "baptismal splash" (which killed her because she was allergic to water)? Too deep for me.<br /><br />And then, besides the mind-boggling questions stirred up, the author decided to leave huge chunks of the plot out so that the reader just had to guess how the changes in the Witch's character had occurred. Meh. <br /><br />Plus all these random cameo appearances from some Yackle character, who seems to be the reincarnation of a fierce and not entirely pleasant goddess. The nasty old crone shows up just when the Witch's life could use an improvement, and instead helps to set events on the path to yet more doom and despair. Then she utters some cryptic but ominous prophecy and vanishes. Gave me the shudders.<br /><br />Read it if your head needs to be tied in a knot and your heart needs a vigorous massage.<br /><br />Bottom line: I loved it, and I only understood about half of it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14142691447256097039">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1302505304">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11669569067972872686"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.melissa413readsalot.blogspot.com" target="_blank">www.melissa413readsalot.blogspot.com</a><br /><br />I thought this book was good and sad. I thought I had listened to it on tape years ago, but I didn't remember it at all. <br /><br />I felt really sorry for Elphaba (the wicked witch of the west) because from the day she was born (green) she was not liked by her parents or too many people in the world. She was tolerated and a few choice people liked her. It's just like normal society where people are prejudiced against anything different. I'm guessing that would turn a lo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11669569067972872686"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.melissa413readsalot.blogspot.com" target="_blank">www.melissa413readsalot.blogspot.com</a><br /><br />I thought this book was good and sad. I thought I had listened to it on tape years ago, but I didn't remember it at all. <br /><br />I felt really sorry for Elphaba (the wicked witch of the west) because from the day she was born (green) she was not liked by her parents or too many people in the world. She was tolerated and a few choice people liked her. It's just like normal society where people are prejudiced against anything different. I'm guessing that would turn a lot of people wicked. But in my mind she wasn't really wicked. I thought she was awesome for fighting for the animals, as I am a wildlife activist. She always tried to be nice and be friends with people and they just shot her down. <br /><br />I don't understand why her parents loved her younger sister so much. She was born with deformities but she was treated like a queen. It seemed odd to me, but maybe I missed something. Her sister did become the wicked witch of the east. <br /><br />The book was strange in a lot of ways and I found myself thinking "what is going on" throughout a lot of the book. Even so, I very much loved reading about Elphaba's birth and life growing up. <br /><br />I look forward to reading the rest of the books in the series. I just hope I don't get too lost in those! <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1379.photobucket.com/user/melmartD11/media/book%20banner_zpsh916lrql.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo book banner_zpsh916lrql.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437402575i/15581842._SX540_.jpg" /></a></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RfpnTakRtGUKRWLzlVzymfCwuTNbaE4ULV1ziyTqOEMgLlieW8Ogn4adFkjrPRQruUd6KhK1Cb5HVHYZ6H/OBg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lOtz1/a5rhBLlGv14saKDe0PrZfQl6eHfrp8Ovef81/xP0wEBGu66sdMH06cp2y/pPhujplK4C0Us3moOwoFGg==')})

The most successful musical on Broadway in 2004, Wicked is based on the bestselling novel of the same name by Gregory Maguire. It tells the story of Elphaba, the headstrong Wicked Witch of the West, and Glinda, the good witch, growing up in the Land of Oz. The show has cast a spell on fans, many of whom return for second and third viewings. In 2005, the show begins an extensive tour across the United States and Canada, hitting major cities such as Toronto, Chicago, Los Angeles, San Francisco, Washington, D.C., and many more. This beautifully packaged, whimsical keepsake is designed to resemble the Grimmerie, an ancient book of spells that Elphaba uses in the show. Wicked: The Grimmerie offers fans a behind-the-curtains peek at the musical, profiles of the cast and creative team, and inside stories, with full-color photographs throughout. A prequel to the all-American classic The Wizard of Oz, this new musical is a character study of Elphaba and Glinda, school roommates who grow up to become the Wicked Witch and the Good Witch, respectively. This book is a Piano/Vocal Selections book, in standard piano vocal format with the melody in the piano part. It features a special section of color photos from the production, a note from composer/lyricist Stephen Schwartz, and these fantastic tunes: As Long as You\'re Mine Dancing Through Life Defying Gravity For Good I Couldn\'t Be Happier I\'m Not That Girl No Good Deed No One Mourns the Wicked One Short Day Popular What Is This Feeling? He owns a building in the heart of San Francisco, and runs a secondhand store with the help of a couple of loyal, if marginally insane, employees. He\'s married to a bright and pretty woman who actually loves him for his normalcy. And she, Rachel, is about to have their first child.\n\nYes, Charlie\'s doing okay for a Beta. That is, until the day his daughter, Sophie, is born. Just as Charlie -- exhausted from the birth -- turns to go home, he sees a strange man in mint-green golf wear at Rachel\'s hospital bedside, a man who claims that no one should be able to see him. But see him Charlie does, and from here on out, things get really weird. . . .\n\nPeople start dropping dead around him, giant ravens perch on his building, and it seems that everywhere he goes, a dark presence whispers to him from under the streets. Strange names start appearing on his nightstand notepad, and before he knows it, those people end up dead, too. Yup, it seems that Charlie Asher has been recruited for a new job, an unpleasant but utterly necessary one: Death. It\'s a dirty job. But hey, somebody\'s gotta do it.\n\nChristopher Moore, the man whose Lamb served up Jesus\' &amp;quot;missing years&amp;quot; (with the funny parts left in), and whose Fluke found the deep humor in whale researchers\' lives, now shines his comic light on the undiscovered country we all eventually explore -- death and dying -- and the results are hilarious, heartwarming, and a hell of a lot of     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7025.Gregory_Maguire">About Gregory Maguire</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/7025.Gregory_Maguire"><img alt="7025" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1319068553p2/7025.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer13856260781312713811">Gregory Maguire is an American author, whose novels are revisionist retellings of children's stories (such as L. Frank Baum's <em>The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</em> into <em>Wicked</em>). He received his Ph.D. in English and American Literature from Tufts University, and his B.A. from the State University of New York at Albany. He was a professor and co-director at the Simmons College Center for the Study of Children'</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13856260781312713811">Gregory Maguire is an American author, whose novels are revisionist retellings of children's stories (such as L. Frank Baum's <em>The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</em> into <em>Wicked</em>). He received his Ph.D. in English and American Literature from Tufts University, and his B.A. from the State University of New York at Albany. He was a professor and co-director at the Simmons College Center for the Study of Children's Literature from 1979-1985. In 1987 he co-founded Children's Literature New England (a non-profit educational charity).<p>Maguire has served as artist-in-residence at the Blue Mountain Center, the Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum, and the Hambidge Center. He lives in Concord, Massachusetts.</p></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13856260781312713811">...more</a>

    </div>

A Lion Among Men chronicles a battle of wits hastened by the Emerald City\'s approaching armies.
